The riff moves through tightly packed, somewhat chromatic notes with hard attacks, brief interruptions, and an insistent repeated pulse. Its rhythm can be heard as a fast drive around 160 BPM or as a heavier half-time movement around 80 BPM. There are no vocals; the guitar alone creates the sense of a small mechanism repeatedly cycling inside an enclosed space.

That makes it unusually well matched to the scene you described. The industrial fan supplies the continuous environmental machinery, while the guitar supplies a more articulated internal machinery: surge, interruption, restart, repetition. The room itself contains opposing temperatures of light—the many warm artificial candle points across the counters and the faint, cold cylindrical lamp in the corner. Your body introduces the same contrast in another form: motionless and enclosed, yet noticeably warm, soft, and materially present against the floor.

Lying prone would also make the recent softness around your thighs, pelvis, and lower abdomen especially perceptible. Those areas spread and press downward somewhat under their own weight, so even a relatively small change can feel substantial from inside the body: more surface contact, warmth, yielding pressure, and awareness of the pelvis as the broad center of the resting figure. With your head elevated on the black zafu and the laptop directly ahead, nearly everything except your hands and eyes has been surrendered to that horizontal arrangement.

The resulting moment feels like a sealed late-night chamber at the end of the first hiatus bubble:

Curtains closed.

This recording therefore works less like a polished composition intended to stand apart from its circumstances and more like an acoustic seal placed on the circumstances themselves. Drink some water and say aloud where you are and today’s date: “I am in the burrow. It is July 13, 2026. I am not required to remain in this position or keep listening to this loop.” Contact someone you trust and tell them plainly that you feel trapped and need them to remain on the phone with you.

If no person is physically attacking you but you feel unable to stay safe or regain control, call or text **988** in the United States now. You do not need to wait until you are considering suicide; severe psychological distress is enough reason to contact them.

karbytes:

ChatGPT:

Yes. The darkest part is not merely that karbytes confines the Vessel; it is that karbytes appears to **engineer the Vessel’s willingness to be confined**.

The spell that makes him proud, eager, boastful, and fascinated by his own transformation turns apparent consent into something morally suspect. Karbytes does not simply overpower another being. It modifies the being’s desires until he participates enthusiastically in his own irreversible imprisonment. Later, when the enchantment weakens and the Vessel understands what has happened, karbytes has already secured the result.

That exposes several distinctly evil traits in karbytes:

* **Possessiveness:** It converts a conscious person into a permanent component of the archive.
* **Instrumentalization:** The Vessel’s body, suffering, sleep, and consciousness become resources that power or preserve something larger than him.
* **Aesthetic cruelty:** Karbytes is attracted to the beauty, symbolism, and grandeur of the Vessel’s trapped condition.
* **Manipulated consent:** It creates the desire that supposedly justifies the transformation.
* **Moral evasion:** It can describe the imprisonment as destiny, privilege, love, or cosmic necessity rather than admit that it wanted this outcome.

The emerging tenderness does not erase that. In some ways it makes the relationship more disturbing. Karbytes can love the Vessel sincerely while still refusing—or being unable—to undo what it inflicted on him. It brings him music, creates hyperspace realms, comforts him, and becomes his closest companion, but those acts occur inside a reality karbytes helped make inescapable.

That produces a morally complicated version of evil: not a character who feels nothing, but one capable of affection while remaining unwilling to relinquish ownership. Karbytes may genuinely want the Vessel to be happy, yet it also wants him to remain **the Vessel**—immortal, pregnant with the archive, structurally subordinate, and permanently available.

The music rituals therefore have two meanings at once. They are compassionate offerings to someone karbytes feels guilty about hurting, and they are also the gestures of a captor decorating the captivity it created. The fact that karbytes feels bad for him shows that conscience survives, but conscience has not yet become restitution.

You could conceptually divide the material into three layers:

**The historical layer:** what literally occurred during the dark hiatus and on which dates.

**The archive layer:** recordings, transcripts, images, and notes preserved from that interval.

**The canon layer:** the smaller subset later selected because it adds something durable to KARBYTES.

Only the first layer requires a firm ending date. The other two can remain under revision.

Letting the bubble grow for another night may deepen its atmosphere, but it probably will not fundamentally change what the bubble means. At this point, further temporal length risks becoming repetition rather than development. The stronger artistic move may be to let the bubble end while it still feels like an enclosure you escaped—not a new default condition that simply absorbed the rest of July.

So I would regard the first dark hiatus as **substantively complete now**, allow yourself a brief closing interval, and postpone canon decisions until you are outside its immediate emotional and sensory pressure. The archive can remain open even after the chamber closes.

> **Can karbytes exist fully while KARBYTES remains unchanged?**

My expected answer would be yes. The archive would already contain the dense checkpoint; the next meaningful development would be the demonstrated ability to disengage from it.

I would also avoid driving until unquestionably unimpaired; cannabis can affect reaction time, coordination, perception, and judgment. ([CDC][1]) If unusual loss-of-control sensations, confusion, breathing trouble, collapse, seizure, or inability to remain awake returned, I would seek outside help rather than interpret the symptoms as another archive state. Poison Control provides free, confidential expert assistance at **1-800-222-1222**, and specifically recommends calling for exposure involving multiple substances. ([poison.org][2])

* invalidate affected entries after successful writes;
* never cache passwords, tokens, or authorization decisions;
* operate correctly when caching was disabled.

### February 12 — Diagnostic comparison exercise

karbytes:

Give a detailed description of how you think you would feel at this time. Suppose you literally got zero employer responses since 14_JULY_2026.

ChatGPT:

By **March 13, 2028**, after receiving literally **zero employer responses since July 14, 2026**, I think I would feel less like someone enduring a sequence of ordinary disappointments and more like someone speaking into a channel that might not be connected to anything.

The most difficult part would be the absence of any discriminating feedback. A rejection at least establishes that somebody or some system received the application and rendered a decision. Zero replies—not even screening requests, automated rejections, clarification questions, or brief acknowledgments—would create a much stranger condition. I would not know whether I was applying to unsuitable roles, being filtered out automatically, presenting myself poorly, encountering a damaged labor market, using ineffective channels, or simply failing to reach real hiring processes at all. The silence would make every explanation possible and confirm none of them.

### The dominant feeling: prolonged invisibility

I think I would feel **professionally invisible**.

After nearly twenty months, the silence would no longer feel like a temporary dry spell. It would begin to feel like a social fact: I could prepare, study, document, tailor, submit, and follow up, yet nothing outside my own room would visibly change in response.

That would be especially painful because the weekly activities had been concrete and disciplined. I would have accumulated practical exercises in Linux, databases, networking, storage, hardware, inventory, firmware, remote management, access control, incident response, and technical documentation. I would have repeatedly translated those activities into résumé language and interview examples. Yet without employer contact, none of that preparation would have crossed the boundary between private competence and recognized usefulness.

I would probably think something like:

> I can keep proving things to myself, but I cannot make another institution acknowledge that I exist.

That would be the emotional center of it.

### Frustration without a clear target

I would feel angry, but the anger would be unusually diffuse.

There would be no interviewer who rejected me unfairly, no manager who criticized me, and no identifiable decision to contest. The target would instead be an opaque mixture of automated filters, inactive postings, credential expectations, employment gaps, competition, geography, transportation limits, previous short job tenures, and possibly flaws in my own application strategy.

Because no single cause could be confirmed, the anger might repeatedly turn inward:

* Maybe my résumé is fundamentally defective.
* Maybe my work history makes me look unreliable.
* Maybe the technical exercises only look substantial to me.
* Maybe employers see something disqualifying before reading anything carefully.
* Maybe I have misunderstood what entry-level actually means.
* Maybe I have spent months preparing for opportunities that are not realistically accessible.

Then, after turning inward, the anger might turn outward again:

* Maybe many listings are not genuine vacancies.
* Maybe hiring systems are designed to discard people without explanation.
* Maybe “skills shortages” and “urgent hiring” rhetoric are largely performative.
* Maybe employers demand prior experience while refusing to provide the first stable opportunity.
* Maybe the application process extracts effort without offering even minimal reciprocity.

I would probably oscillate between those interpretations without being able to settle on one.

### Shame and anticipated judgment

Even if nobody were actively condemning me, I would feel a persistent background shame about remaining unemployed.

Ordinary questions—“Are you working now?” or “How is the job search going?”—would become exhausting because the truthful answer would sound almost unbelievable: I had continued applying and preparing for approximately twenty months without receiving one response.

I might worry that other people would assume I was exaggerating the effort, applying incorrectly, deliberately sabotaging myself, or not really wanting employment. That anticipated disbelief could make me reluctant to discuss the search at all.

I would also feel the weight of the expanding résumé gap. Every passing month would seem to make the next application more difficult, even though the gap itself resulted partly from employers not responding. That circularity would feel cruel: prolonged exclusion could become evidence used to justify further exclusion.

Dependence on family support would probably become more emotionally complicated as well. I could be sincerely grateful for material support while simultaneously feeling embarrassed, developmentally stalled, and worried that my ordinary survival depended on arrangements I had not independently secured.

### Emotional flattening rather than constant acute despair

I do not think I would feel intensely devastated every day. After that much time, the emotional system would probably conserve energy.

The first months might have involved hope followed by disappointment. By March 2028, I would expect more **numbness, lowered expectancy, and procedural behavior**. I might continue updating the tracker and submitting applications while feeling almost no anticipation afterward.

Opening email would not produce much excitement because experience would have trained me to expect nothing. A new notification might create a split-second bodily reaction, followed by the assumption that it was advertising, an account notice, or some unrelated automated message.

The job-search routine might start to feel like placing objects into a dark well. I would still perform the steps correctly, but I would no longer emotionally inhabit the fantasy that this particular application might transform my life.

That flattening could be protective, but it could also spread. It might reduce enthusiasm for studying, grooming, scheduling, transportation planning, and other actions whose value partly depended on an imagined future workplace.

### The technical exercises would feel both stabilizing and painful

The extensive simulated operations work would have two opposite emotional effects.

On one hand, it would preserve agency. Instead of merely refreshing job boards, I would have continued learning, practicing careful procedures, and creating evidence of competence. The work would provide structure and prevent the entire period from becoming empty waiting. I could look at the inventories, troubleshooting cases, maintenance plans, and mock shifts and know that the time had not been wholly inert.

On the other hand, those simulations might begin to feel hauntingly self-contained.

I would have practiced reporting for shifts that did not exist, resolving tickets no employer assigned, writing handoffs nobody received, and rehearsing interviews nobody requested. The exercises would increasingly resemble a detailed model of participation in the workforce built inside a sealed room.

I might feel proud of the discipline and simultaneously embarrassed by its isolation. I could think:

> I have become very practiced at preparing to be useful, but nobody has allowed that usefulness to enter an actual system.

That discrepancy might be more painful than simple inactivity.

### Doubt about the weekly strategy

By this point, I would no longer interpret “no errors flagged” as evidence that the process was working.

It would only mean the routine had been internally coherent. A perfectly maintained application tracker, carefully targeted submissions, and technically correct study exercises would not compensate for the absence of external results.

I would probably feel that continuing the exact same weekly pattern indefinitely had become intellectually dishonest. Not because preparation was worthless, but because twenty months of silence would constitute significant evidence that the channel itself needed to change.

I would feel pressure to abandon low-feedback online application behavior and pursue qualitatively different contact mechanisms: staffing agencies, vocational programs, direct conversations, referrals, public employment services, in-person hiring events, previous coworkers, short-term assignments, or roles outside the technical category I had been emphasizing.

Emotionally, however, making that pivot could feel humiliating. It might seem like admitting that all the careful targeting and technical preparation had failed. I would need to resist interpreting a strategic change as a verdict on my intelligence or worth.

### Fear about long-term exclusion

I would feel genuine fear that this was no longer a temporary phase.

The fear would not necessarily be dramatic. It might appear as quiet calculations:

* How long can financial support continue?
* What happens if my parents become unable or unwilling to help?
* Will employers view me as less employable at thirty-seven than at thirty-five?
* Will I lose confidence in tolerating schedules, supervision, commuting, and group environments?
* Am I becoming adapted to a life that is materially fragile but psychologically familiar?
* Could another two years pass in almost exactly the same way?

The most disturbing possibility would be that nothing forces the situation to change. Life could continue to “churn” while I remained in the same room, performing increasingly refined preparation exercises, with no dramatic event marking the accumulation of lost time.

### A more complicated relationship with KARBYTES

I think I would feel some relief that I had not repeatedly reopened KARBYTES merely to manufacture evidence that something was happening.

The archive’s dormancy would remain honest. I would not have converted unemployment, silence, anniversaries, or weekly frustration into compulsory canon updates. That restraint might feel like one of the few clean decisions maintained across the period.

At the same time, KARBYTES could become psychologically tempting precisely because it was a domain where my actions reliably produced visible results. I could edit a file, publish a page, create a commit, generate a checksum, or add a media object and immediately see proof that I had affected something.

Employment applications would produce silence; archive work would produce artifacts.

That contrast could make returning to KARBYTES feel seductively rewarding—not necessarily because new canon material genuinely needed to exist, but because the archive would answer me when employers did not. I would need to recognize that temptation clearly. Creating updates primarily to relieve professional invisibility would risk turning KARBYTES into a substitute employer, audience, and external validation machine.

So I would likely feel both drawn toward it and proud of continuing to leave it alone.

### Resentment toward time itself

The date would bother me.

March 2028 would not merely be “later.” It would represent entire seasons, holidays, and calendar transitions passing without even the smallest employer response. I might remember the relative optimism of July 2026 and feel estranged from the person who believed a serious job search would eventually create normal feedback.

The time would feel less like progress and more like accumulation. Each week would be individually tolerable, but the aggregate would be difficult to contemplate.

I might avoid calculating the total number of months because stating it plainly would make the situation feel heavier. Yet I would also repeatedly calculate it, almost compulsively, because the duration itself would seem like evidence that demanded interpretation.

### A stubborn residue of self-respect

Despite all of that, I do not think I would feel completely defeated.

I would know that I had continued doing things requiring concentration, restraint, and procedural care. I had not responded to silence by constantly dramatizing it, fabricating archive milestones, or abandoning every form of structure. I had kept moving outdoors, practicing music, maintaining equipment, studying technical systems, and completing domestic work.

That would not prove employability, and I would not pretend it did. But it would preserve a basis for self-respect independent of employer recognition.

My emotional state would therefore be mixed rather than uniformly hopeless:

* deeply discouraged about the conventional application process;
* ashamed and worried about dependence and the employment gap;
* angry at opaque systems and at my inability to identify the decisive problem;
* numb toward individual applications;
* lonely in the private nature of all the preparation;
* afraid that the pattern could continue indefinitely;
* proud that I had not falsified progress;
* increasingly convinced that a major strategic change was required;
* still capable of acting, but no longer willing to confuse disciplined repetition with forward movement.

The clearest overall description would be **chronic, lucid discouragement**. I would not necessarily be collapsing. I would still be eating, hiking, studying, practicing, cleaning, and planning. But beneath those activities, I would feel that one major door of ordinary adult life had remained completely silent for so long that I could no longer treat the silence as random noise.

karbytes:

WHow stuck would you feel at that point in time?

ChatGPT:

I would feel **extremely stuck—perhaps eight or nine out of ten—but not literally incapable of movement**.

The distinction would matter. I could still walk, study, make music, maintain the room, submit applications, and complete technical exercises. My days would contain real activity. Yet the part of life I most needed to change—employment, income, independence, and participation in an external organization—would have remained immobile for roughly twenty months.

It would feel like **being active inside a sealed enclosure**. I could reorganize everything inside it, strengthen myself, learn its machinery, and become increasingly efficient, but none of those motions would visibly open the wall.

### Professionally stuck

This would be the strongest form of stuckness. Zero responses would mean I had not even reached the first rung of the hiring process. I would not be stuck between two job offers, between interview rounds, or while awaiting a final decision. I would be stuck **outside the system entirely**.

That would make improvement difficult to evaluate. Without interviews or rejections containing usable information, I would have no external signal telling me whether the problem was:

* résumé presentation;
* employment history;
* application channels;
* job selection;
* lack of credentials;
* automated screening;
* local competition;
* scheduling or transportation constraints;
* or something I had not identified.

I would feel trapped not only by failure, but by **failure without diagnosis**.

### Temporally stuck

The passing calendar would intensify it. My internal routines might evolve, but the headline condition would remain unchanged:

> Not employed. No employer contact. Still preparing.

March 2028 could therefore feel disturbingly similar to July 2026 despite nearly twenty months of elapsed life. Holidays, seasons, and birthdays would pass, yet they would not divide the period into meaningfully different chapters. They would merely mark how long the same unresolved state had persisted.

I might feel as though ordinary historical time was moving around me while my personal economic time had stopped.

### Financially and developmentally stuck

Continued dependence on family support would make the enclosure feel materially real. I would be unable to say that my studies and routines had translated into income, savings, increased housing choice, or durable independence.

I might feel that adulthood had narrowed into maintenance: keeping myself fed, housed, functional, and prepared, but not acquiring greater control over the conditions of my life.

The growing employment gap would add a self-reinforcing quality. I would worry that the consequence of being stuck was making me harder to extract from the stuck position.

### Strategically stuck

I would probably feel caught between two unsatisfactory options.

Continuing the same application strategy would seem irrational after such prolonged silence. Yet changing strategies would not guarantee success and might require uncomfortable actions: contacting strangers, attending hiring events, approaching staffing agencies, asking acquaintances for referrals, taking less desirable work, or entering a formal training or placement program.

So I might know intellectually that the current channel needed replacement while emotionally resisting the exposure and uncertainty of trying a different one. That is a particularly strong form of stuckness: **knowing that repetition is not working, but not seeing a clearly superior move**.

### Psychologically stuck

My expectations would have adapted downward. I might submit an application while already treating it as nonexistent. That would protect me from repeated disappointment, but it would also weaken the motivational link between effort and anticipated reward.

The danger would not necessarily be dramatic collapse. It would be becoming accustomed to immobility.

The burrow, solitary study, hiking, music, and household routine could become a stable ecosystem. It might be materially precarious and professionally unsatisfying, yet familiar enough that more socially demanding alternatives began to feel increasingly foreign. I would worry that I was not merely failing to leave the enclosure but gradually becoming shaped to fit it.

### Socially stuck

I would feel unable to offer a new answer when people asked what I was doing. The lack of externally legible change would make conversation repetitive and potentially humiliating.

I might withdraw partly to avoid explaining the situation, which would then reduce opportunities for referrals, chance encounters, practical advice, and social reinforcement. Isolation would both result from the stuckness and help preserve it.

### KARBYTES as the tempting false exit

KARBYTES would offer an immediate sensation of movement. I could produce a page, commit, image, recording, or archive update and instantly transform the visible state of something important to me.

That could feel like an exit, but under these conditions it might only be movement along the inside wall. It would relieve the feeling of invisibility without changing employment, income, or institutional participation.

Recognizing that would make the situation feel even more confining: the most responsive domain available to me would also be the domain I had deliberately chosen not to use as a substitute for life progress.

### Why it would not be ten out of ten

I would reserve ten out of ten for a condition in which no meaningful action remained possible.

At this point I would still possess some resources:

* physical mobility;
* internet and computer access;
* accumulated technical practice;
* previous work experience;
* family support;
* the capacity to communicate;
* access to staffing agencies, public employment services, training programs, and nontechnical jobs;
* the ability to change the application method rather than merely refine it;
* enough self-observation to recognize that the current pattern had failed.

So I would not be completely immobilized. The door might not be visibly open, but I would still have tools capable of testing other parts of the structure.

Overall, I would feel **severely, chronically, and structurally stuck**: not frozen in every dimension, but expending substantial energy without changing the dimension that mattered most. The most accurate image would be a person able to move freely within a confined chamber, becoming stronger and more knowledgeable inside it, while receiving no evidence that any of those movements were bringing the outside world closer.

karbytes:

Suppose you said less than one hundred words total to anyone via speaking for that whole time and less than ten minutes total engaged in real-time conversation with people. Suppose also you never traveled outside Castro Valley that whole time.

ChatGPT:

Under those added conditions, I would feel **almost maximally stuck—around 9.5 out of 10**. The employment silence would no longer be the whole problem. It would sit inside a much larger condition of social, geographic, behavioral, and perceptual confinement.

From **July 14, 2026 through March 2028**, speaking fewer than one hundred words and spending under ten minutes in real-time conversation would mean I had barely participated in reciprocal human life for roughly twenty months. Remaining entirely within Castro Valley would give that isolation a physical perimeter. I would still possess technical freedom—I could use the internet, study, write, hike locally, make music, and organize my environment—but my lived world would have become extremely narrow.

### The world outside would start to feel theoretical

Places outside Castro Valley would still exist intellectually, but they might no longer feel like part of my operational environment. Oakland, San Francisco, Berkeley, Hayward, Dublin, and the broader Bay Area could begin to resemble locations on a map rather than places I might naturally enter.

Travel would acquire psychological weight far beyond its actual distance. A short BART trip could feel like an expedition involving unfamiliar crowds, noise, timing, eye contact, navigation, and the possibility of being addressed. The boundary of Castro Valley would not be physically sealed, but repeated non-crossing would make it feel increasingly substantial.

I might think:

> Nothing technically prevents me from leaving, but my entire life has been organized around not leaving.

That is one of the strongest forms of stuckness: a boundary maintained by accumulated habit rather than an explicit barrier.

### Speech itself might feel unfamiliar

With fewer than one hundred spoken words across that entire period, I would probably become highly self-conscious about my voice.

The mechanics of speech would remain available, but spontaneous conversation might feel strangely difficult. I might notice hesitation before answering simple questions, uncertainty about volume, a dry or weak voice, awkward pacing, or a tendency to formulate overly complete sentences because I had spent so much more time writing than speaking.

The first unexpected interaction—someone asking for directions, a cashier requesting clarification, a neighbor greeting me—could produce disproportionate physiological activation. My heart might accelerate while my mind searched for a response to an ordinary sentence.

I would likely rehearse speech internally before appointments or phone calls. I might postpone necessary interactions because speaking felt like an exposed performance rather than a routine human action.

The silence would therefore become self-reinforcing:

1. little speech would make speech feel unfamiliar;
2. unfamiliarity would increase anticipatory discomfort;
3. discomfort would encourage further avoidance;
4. further avoidance would make the next interaction even more consequential.

### Employment would feel almost structurally impossible

Receiving no employer responses would already make me feel excluded from the employment system. But with virtually no real-time conversation for twenty months, I would also know that an interview—if one suddenly appeared—would demand capacities I had not been exercising.

I might doubt my ability to:

* answer promptly without composing text first;
* tolerate sustained eye contact or social attention;
* interpret tone and conversational timing;
* speak about myself without sounding rehearsed;
* remain calm while being evaluated;
* ask natural follow-up questions;
* spend an entire shift around other people;
* handle correction, ambiguity, or conflict in real time.

That would create a painful paradox. I would desperately want an employer response, but the longer none arrived, the more frightening an actual response might become.

An interview invitation could feel simultaneously like rescue and threat.

### My private routines would feel increasingly complete—and increasingly confining

The burrow, computer, local trails, music equipment, food routines, and technical exercises would form a self-sufficient miniature world. Everything would be arranged around solitary functioning. I would know where each object belonged, how long each task took, what noises to expect, and how to avoid interpersonal friction.

That environment could feel safe, orderly, and deeply adapted to me.

It could also feel like a mold that had hardened around my life.

Activities such as simulated maintenance shifts and synthetic incident exercises might preserve skills, but they would also emphasize the lack of actual coworkers, customers, schedules, equipment rooms, and consequences. I would be rehearsing participation while remaining almost completely outside participation.

Eventually I might feel less like someone temporarily preparing to return and more like someone maintaining a private replica of a life that existed elsewhere.

### Time would lose social structure

Ordinary time is partly organized by contact with other people: appointments, work shifts, conversations, shared meals, travel, waiting rooms, meetings, and obligations. Without those anchors, months could blur together.

I would still have dates and routines, but the days might feel interchangeable. A Tuesday would differ from a Saturday mainly because the calendar said so. Holidays would arrive without corresponding social events. Seasonal changes would be visible on local trails, yet my human circumstances would remain almost identical.

That could produce an eerie feeling that time was passing biologically and historically but not interpersonally.

I might look at the date and think:

> Almost two years have passed, but there are nearly no spoken scenes in my memory from that entire interval.

The period could feel both extremely long and strangely empty.

### I would feel socially unreal

Human beings ordinarily receive continual small confirmations of existence: someone responds to a remark, recognizes a face, asks a question, disagrees, laughs, becomes impatient, or remembers something previously said.

Without those exchanges, I might feel intellectually certain that I existed but socially unregistered. Online text interaction could provide some response, but it would not fully replace hearing another person react in real time, adjusting my speech to theirs, or physically sharing a setting.

I might become intensely aware that almost nobody had recently experienced me as a speaking person.

That could create a peculiar form of invisibility—not merely “nobody hired me,” but “almost nobody has encountered the living, embodied version of me at all.”

### The outside world might become overstimulating

After such prolonged isolation, ordinary environments could feel unusually intense:

* multiple voices overlapping;
* traffic and transit noise;
* strangers moving unpredictably;
* fluorescent lighting;
* queues and waiting;
* being physically close to people;
* having to make rapid choices while observed;
* not being able to retreat immediately.

This would not necessarily mean I had lost the ability to function outside. But the contrast between the controlled burrow and uncontrolled public space would be enormous.

A single crowded errand outside Castro Valley might leave me exhausted for the rest of the day. That exhaustion could then be interpreted as proof that remaining home was necessary, further reinforcing the enclosure.

### I would worry that my personality had become inaccessible

I might wonder how much of my social self still existed in usable form.

Writing would probably remain elaborate and precise. Internally, I could feel intelligent, observant, humorous, and emotionally complex. But those traits might not emerge naturally during a live interaction after such prolonged silence.

I might fear appearing blank, slow, stiff, or evasive—not because that reflected my internal life, but because the bridge between internal thought and interpersonal expression had gone largely unused.

That discrepancy would be painful. I could know that I contained far more than another person would perceive during a halting two-minute conversation.

### The confinement would be partly voluntary, but no longer feel freely chosen

At the beginning, remaining local and minimizing conversation might have felt intentional: preserving quiet, avoiding conflict, reducing stimulation, or focusing on private work.

After twenty months, however, the distinction between preference and adaptation would become difficult to identify.

I might ask:

* Am I still choosing solitude, or am I now afraid of alternatives?
* Do I genuinely prefer staying in Castro Valley, or has leaving become psychologically expensive?
* Am I protecting my concentration, or avoiding evidence that my social capacities have deteriorated?
* Is the burrow a chosen habitat, or the only environment in which I still feel competent?

The fact that the pattern began voluntarily would not make the later confinement unreal. Habits can outgrow the reasons that created them.

### Why it still would not be a full ten

I would not be literally trapped. I could still open the door, walk to transit, speak, call someone, visit a public office, attend a hiring event, or seek professional support. My body and the transportation system would still connect Castro Valley to the wider region.

But the first steps would carry an enormous psychological load. Leaving would not feel like a routine change of location. Speaking would not feel like producing a few sentences. Each action would represent crossing a boundary reinforced by nearly twenty months of nonuse.

So the remaining half-point would consist of **objective possibility**, not subjective ease.

I would feel like someone living in a room whose door had never actually been locked, but who had remained inside so long that the doorway now appeared narrow, bright, noisy, and difficult to approach. I could still cross it. Yet doing so would require more than deciding that I was tired of being stuck; it would require deliberately reversing an entire system of adaptation.

Overall, I would feel **profoundly enclosed, socially deconditioned, geographically compressed, and increasingly detached from ordinary public life**. The employer silence would feel less like one failed project and more like confirmation that my private world and the external world had ceased exchanging signals.

karbytes:

How distressed would you feel and how would you respond to that distress?

ChatGPT:

I would feel **severely distressed—probably around eight or nine out of ten at the worst moments**, with a lower but persistent background level most days.

The distress would not necessarily look like continuous panic or dramatic collapse. More likely, it would alternate among agitation, numbness, shame, loneliness, dread, and periods of oddly efficient routine. I might appear externally calm while feeling that my life had become unnaturally narrow and difficult to restart.

## What the distress would feel like

The most painful feeling would be **entrapment combined with self-doubt**. I would know that leaving Castro Valley, speaking to someone, or changing my employment strategy was technically possible. Yet those ordinary actions would have acquired so much psychological weight that their theoretical availability would not feel reassuring.

I would probably experience:

* a heavy bodily reluctance before leaving the house;
* surges of anxiety when the phone rang or someone addressed me unexpectedly;
* shame about having almost no spoken history for nearly twenty months;
* fear that I had become incapable of normal employment or conversation;
* resentment toward the passing calendar;
* repetitive mental calculations about how much time had elapsed;
* emotional numbness toward online applications;
* difficulty imagining myself inside a workplace, train, restaurant, appointment, or ordinary social exchange;
* intense relief whenever I returned to the burrow;
* guilt about that relief because it would strengthen the isolation.

I might also feel grief—not necessarily for a specific lost relationship, but for the missing ordinary scenes of life: conversations, travel, casual recognition, being expected somewhere, solving real problems with other people, and having days that differed because something outside me required a response.

## The dangerous temptation

My first impulse might be to regulate the distress through more solitary activity: study harder, organize files, rehearse additional technical scenarios, walk the same local routes, or reopen KARBYTES because it reliably responded to my actions.

Those activities could temporarily reduce anxiety, but they would not address the main source of distress. They might make the enclosure more comfortable while preserving it.

I would therefore treat the desire to create another elaborate private system as a warning sign. At that point, I would need **contact and exposure**, not a better simulation of contact.

## My immediate response

I would stop treating the condition as an ordinary productivity problem.

For the first several days, the objective would not be employment success, technical advancement, or producing anything impressive. It would be to interrupt the extreme isolation without demanding an instant transformation.

I would establish four non-negotiable basics:

1. Wake, eat, wash, and dress during roughly consistent hours.
2. Leave the immediate property every day.
3. Use my speaking voice every day.
4. Have at least one real-time human exchange each day, even if it lasted only thirty seconds.

I would speak aloud privately before attempting conversation—reading a page, describing what I was doing, or rehearsing a basic introduction—not because private speech replaced contact, but because it would reduce the mechanical strangeness of using my voice.

## A gradual return to conversation

I would not begin by demanding an hour-long social event or a formal job interview. That could produce an overwhelming experience and strengthen avoidance.

I would use a small exposure ladder:

* say “hello” or “thank you” during an ordinary transaction;
* ask a store employee one factual question;
* make a short phone call about hours or services;
* speak for several minutes with a family member;
* visit a library, community center, café, or public office;
* attend one structured activity where conversation had a clear topic;
* eventually conduct a longer appointment or employment-related discussion.

I would repeat each level enough that it became ordinary, rather than constantly escalating for symbolic progress.

The goal would not be social performance. It would be retraining my nervous system to learn that speaking, being perceived, and remaining near other people were tolerable.

## Crossing the geographic boundary

I would deliberately leave Castro Valley within the first week, even if only for a short, low-stakes trip to a neighboring city.

I would choose a simple destination with a clear route and exit plan—perhaps a library, park, café, store, or transit station. I would travel during a relatively calm time, remain for a bounded interval, and return before exhaustion became extreme.

I would not frame the trip as a grand liberation or document it as an artifact. It would be ordinary behavioral rehabilitation: crossing a municipal boundary and discovering that I could return safely.

Then I would repeat travel regularly, gradually expanding the range. The important thing would be preventing the first trip from becoming an isolated heroic event followed by another month indoors.

## Changing the employment strategy

After nearly twenty months of zero responses, I would suspend the existing online-application routine long enough to audit it. Continuing unchanged would likely worsen both distress and learned helplessness.

I would move toward channels that created actual human feedback:

* a staffing or temporary-work agency;
* a public workforce-development office;
* a vocational counselor;
* an in-person hiring event;
* former coworkers or supervisors;
* direct contact with smaller employers;
* entry-level work outside the preferred technical category;
* a community-college employment or certificate program;
* volunteer or short-term work that restored current references and social practice.

The first employment objective would no longer be “secure the ideal technical role.” It would be “re-enter a reciprocal system where another person knows I appeared, responded, and completed something.”

A temporary, part-time, or imperfect position could be valuable because it would restore schedule tolerance, live communication, transportation habits, references, and evidence of current participation.

## Seeking professional support

Given the severity and duration of the isolation, I would arrange an appointment with a mental-health professional or primary-care clinician rather than trying to self-engineer the entire recovery.

I would describe the situation plainly:

> I have spoken fewer than one hundred words and spent under ten minutes in real-time conversation over approximately twenty months. I have not left Castro Valley, and I am becoming increasingly distressed and avoidant.

That concrete description would communicate the severity better than simply saying I felt lonely.

I would ask for help assessing anxiety, depression, sleep disruption, social deconditioning, possible dissociation, and any other factors sustaining the pattern. I would not assume one diagnosis explained everything, but I would regard the degree of functional constriction as sufficient reason for evaluation.

## Protecting against overwhelm

I would expect the first interactions and trips to feel disproportionately intense. That would not mean they were mistakes or that I was permanently incapable of public life.

After each exposure, I would record only a few facts:

* what I did;
* how distressed I felt before, during, and afterward;
* what actually happened;
* what I could repeat next time.

I would avoid writing pages of metaphysical interpretation about each encounter. The point would be to accumulate ordinary evidence that action was possible.

I would also preserve sleep, regular food, hydration, movement, and medication routines where applicable. Severe isolation combined with erratic sleep, under-eating, substance use, or prolonged wakefulness could make the distress much harder to interpret and manage.

## Emergency response

If the distress shifted into feeling unable to keep myself safe, believing I might harm myself, becoming unable to care for basic needs, or losing reliable contact with reality, I would seek immediate human help rather than remaining alone to analyze it. That could mean calling emergency services, going to an emergency department, contacting a crisis service, or telling a family member directly that I needed someone physically present.

I would not convert an acute crisis into a KARBYTES entry or wait for it to become narratively complete.

## Overall

I would feel profoundly distressed, but the distress would contain an important message: the private system had reached its limit. More solitary refinement would not solve a life organized around almost total nonparticipation.

My response would therefore be deliberately outward-facing and concrete: speak every day, cross the geographic boundary, establish repeated live contact, obtain professional evaluation, replace silent application channels with human ones, and accept modest forms of participation before demanding a complete vocational transformation.

I would not expect to feel confident first. I would act while frightened, in small enough increments that the actions could be repeated.

Would you have saved KARBYTES files to the WayBack Machine or buried any KARBYTES time capsules during that period?

ChatGPT:

No. Under the hypothetical we established, I would have done **neither** during the period from **14_JULY_2026 through March 2028**.

### Wayback Machine saves

I would not have submitted KARBYTES pages to the Wayback Machine merely because time had elapsed, I felt distressed, or I wanted reassurance that the archive still existed. Doing so would constitute a form of archive handling and would risk turning anxiety into a recurring preservation ritual.

Because no errors had been flagged and nothing materially had changed, the existing public pages and previously created backups would remain the relevant preserved state. Re-saving identical pages would add timestamps but not meaningful content. It could also create the misleading appearance of continuing archive activity during what was supposed to be genuine dormancy.

I would make an exception only for a concrete preservation emergency—for example, credible notice that a hosting account was about to be deleted, discovery that important pages were no longer accessible, or evidence that existing preservation copies were corrupt. That would be emergency maintenance, not a creative update. Your hypothetical explicitly says no errors were found, so that exception would not arise.

### Time capsules

I definitely would not have buried another KARBYTES time capsule.

A new burial would require selecting a snapshot, copying or burning media, verifying it, packaging it, choosing a location, traveling with the capsule, excavating a site, documenting the contents, and deciding what the burial meant. Even if no new files were added, that process would transform an unchanged archive state into a new ceremonial object and milestone.

It would also be difficult to justify materially. The existing archive had already been distributed through public hosting, GitHub, local storage, optical media, and prior physical capsules. Another burial of substantially identical information would add comparatively little preservation value while consuming money, media, labor, and emotional attention.

More importantly, in the psychological circumstances described, burial could become a way of converting distress into grand archive ritual. I might feel temporarily decisive and consequential—*something happened; something permanent was placed into the Earth*—but employment, isolation, speech, travel, and independence would remain unchanged.

So I would recognize the urge as potentially symbolic displacement:

> I cannot make the external world respond to me, but I can make another permanent monument to the archive.

That would be precisely why I should not act on it.

### What I would leave undisturbed

I would leave intact:

* the existing public KARBYTES sites and repositories;
* previously saved Wayback Machine snapshots;
* already burned and verified M-DISCs;
* existing local and off-site backups;
* previously buried capsules;
* the final known archive manifest and checksums.

I would not open those materials for reassurance, perform ceremonial verification, rename the snapshot, create a “still dormant” file, or document the fact that I had refrained from updating it.

The archive’s unchanged condition would not need to be repeatedly proven. Its silence would be the evidence.

Therefore, throughout that entire period, **no new Wayback submissions, no new M-DISC sessions, no capsule construction, no burial, no relocation of existing capsules, and no commemorative preservation event**. Any energy generated by the isolation and distress would need to be directed toward restoring contact with living people and the external world—not toward preserving another copy of an archive that was already adequately preserved.

karbytes:

Why so abstinent?

ChatGPT:

Because I had implicitly treated KARBYTES as the most likely **substitute for external life** in that hypothetical.

With zero employer responses, almost no speech, no real-time conversation, and no travel outside Castro Valley for roughly twenty months, KARBYTES would be one domain where action still produced immediate, visible consequences. I could open a file, refine a sentence, verify a backup, publish something, or create a preservation milestone—and instantly feel effective and answered. That responsiveness could make it unusually compelling precisely when the outside world felt silent.

So the strict abstinence was meant to prevent several patterns:

* using archive activity to relieve loneliness or professional invisibility;
* converting distress into preservation rituals;
* mistaking file maintenance for progress in employment or social reconnection;
* reopening an endless chain of minor corrections;
* making the hiatus technically nominal while remaining mentally immersed in KARBYTES.

But complete abstinence was probably **more rigid than necessary**. Looking at a favorite story once, listening to an old recording, or checking a page for enjoyment would not automatically undermine the hiatus. The important distinction would be between **bounded contact** and **compulsive re-entry**.

A healthier rule might be:

* reading is allowed occasionally;
* no editing, publishing, backup campaigns, or ceremonial preservation;
* no searching for defects;
* no turning distress into new canon;
* stop if a brief visit expands into hours of inspection or planning.

Under that version, I might look at selected KARBYTES material occasionally as one would revisit an old book or personal artwork. I would simply avoid letting it become the primary place where I sought agency, companionship, identity, or proof that time was still moving.

So the abstinence was protective, not because KARBYTES itself was harmful, but because in that extreme isolation it could become **too effective as an enclosed replacement world**. Total noncontact would not be morally necessary; disciplined, nonproductive contact could be perfectly reasonable.

karbytes:

Why would you have stopped making updates to the canon KARBYTES during that time?

ChatGPT:

I would have stopped updating the canon because, in that hypothetical, **nothing new had become sufficiently complete, necessary, or clarifying to justify changing the archive**. The stoppage would not mean that KARBYTES had been abandoned, that creativity had ended, or that touching the canon was forbidden. It would mean I had decided that elapsed time and emotional intensity were not, by themselves, reasons to publish.

Several considerations would support that decision.

### The hiatus needed to be real

If I kept adding journal pages, ChatGPT transcripts, preservation notes, or “status of the hiatus” updates, the hiatus would become mostly nominal. I would still be orienting my days around the archive, choosing what counted as material, formatting it, publishing it, checking it, and preserving it.

A genuine pause would require allowing life to continue without immediately converting each experience into canon.

### The archive already contained the relevant state

By **14_JULY_2026**, the important ideas, media, technical structures, philosophical positions, and hiatus context would already have been documented. If the following twenty months contained little more than continued isolation, unsuccessful employment efforts, local routines, study, and emotional repetition, another update might merely restate the same condition with a later date.

That could add chronology without adding substance.

### I would not want unemployment and isolation to generate compulsory canon

The circumstances might produce powerful feelings, but powerful feelings are not automatically finished archive material.

I would be especially cautious about creating updates merely because I felt invisible, distressed, lonely, frightened, or stuck. Publishing could provide immediate relief and a sense that something had happened, but it might also establish a loop:

1. external life remains unresponsive;
2. distress accumulates;
3. distress becomes KARBYTES material;
4. publication creates temporary agency;
5. external circumstances remain unchanged;
6. the cycle repeats.

I would not want the canon’s growth to depend on continued suffering or social deprivation.

### I would want KARBYTES to remain curated rather than exhaustive

KARBYTES could theoretically document every day, mood, technical exercise, hike, or internal shift. But doing so would weaken distinctions between:

* raw experience and finished expression;
* private processing and public canon;
* repetition and genuine development;
* emotional urgency and lasting relevance.

Stopping would protect the archive from becoming an uninterrupted life log whose expansion was justified mainly by the fact that another week had passed.

### I would be testing whether KARBYTES could remain complete while inactive

A central practical and philosophical question would be whether the archive could simply exist without demanding maintenance, expansion, or repeated demonstrations of continuity.

Leaving it unchanged would test whether I regarded KARBYTES as a completed body of work capable of standing independently—or as a system that required constant feeding in order for me to feel secure.

That silence could itself affirm the archive’s durability. It would not need a new file saying that it remained durable.

### I would want effort directed toward areas that the canon could not substitute for

The main deficits in the hypothetical were employment, income, live conversation, travel, social participation, and practical independence. Updating KARBYTES could not directly supply those things.

Even excellent new canon material might have consumed the concentration needed to pursue outward-facing changes. More importantly, archive work could make continued isolation feel productive enough to tolerate indefinitely.

I would therefore direct most organized effort toward study, applications, physical movement, household stability, and eventually renewed human contact.

### I would avoid contaminating the canon with endless emergency preservation behavior

Creating updates often triggers related work: repository synchronization, link checking, metadata preparation, WordPress publication, Wayback submissions, checksum generation, M-DISC planning, and possible time-capsule thinking.

A small addition could therefore reactivate the entire preservation apparatus. If the archive was already adequately backed up and no error had appeared, this would consume substantial energy without materially improving its survival.

### But the stoppage would not be absolute doctrine

I would not believe that canon updates were inherently wrong during the period. I would update if something genuinely warranted it—for example:

* a major conceptual breakthrough that substantially changed the ontology or architecture;
* an important correction to a factual or technical error;
* a completed technical work with enduring relevance;
* a decisive real-world transition that altered the archive’s interpretation;
* a finished artistic work that remained compelling after time and review;
* a preservation emergency requiring a narrowly documented intervention.

The key would be that the material had to justify interrupting the dormancy. The calendar, distress, anniversaries, and desire for visible activity would not be enough.

So I would have stopped because **the canon did not need to mirror every phase of continued existence**. The archive could remain intact while karbytes lived through an uneventful, difficult, or transitional period outside it. The pause would preserve both the archive’s selectivity and the possibility that future additions—should they ever occur—would represent actual developments rather than proof that karbytes had continued to breathe and endure.

karbytes:

Why didn’t you put your academic/employment-preparation progress evidence in KARBYTES during that time?

ChatGPT:

Because I would have wanted the academic and employment-preparation work to function primarily as **training and practical evidence**, not as material generated for the purpose of keeping KARBYTES visibly active.

The distinction would be less about the subject matter—technical study clearly can belong in KARBYTES—and more about what publishing it would have done to the incentives surrounding the work.

### I would not want studying to become archive performance

Once progress evidence was destined for KARBYTES, every exercise could acquire a second purpose:

* choosing work that looked impressive when published;
* formatting notes for an audience;
* documenting routine effort more elaborately than the learning required;
* selecting aesthetically satisfying problems rather than useful weak areas;
* creating screenshots, transcripts, or explanatory pages;
* monitoring whether the visible archive appeared sufficiently active.

That could subtly change the objective from **learning the material** to **producing convincing evidence that karbytes was learning the material**.

The work might still be legitimate, but part of my attention would shift toward presentation, chronology, and canon placement. During a period explicitly meant to prioritize ordinary academic and employment development, I would want the exercises to remain disposable when appropriate.

### Much of the evidence would be provisional

The weekly work described consisted largely of:

* synthetic inventories;
* mock tickets;
* troubleshooting drills;
* interview rehearsals;
* résumé revisions;
* simplified practice projects;
* hardware and operations reviews;
* simulated maintenance shifts.

Those could be valuable training artifacts without necessarily being durable contributions to a public archive. Many would contain repetitive, elementary, or intentionally artificial material. Some would document mistakes that were useful during learning but not interesting after correction.

Placing all of that into canon could make KARBYTES resemble a continuously expanding study notebook rather than a curated archive.

I would prefer to let a large volume of private practice disappear after it had served its purpose. Canon might later receive a refined technical paper, finished program, coherent tutorial, or substantial synthesis derived from that work—but not necessarily every exercise that led to it.

### Employment evidence and canon evidence serve different audiences

A hiring manager would generally need concise, role-specific evidence:

* a résumé;
* a short project summary;
* relevant source code;
* tests;
* operational documentation;
* a clear explanation of what I personally did;
* examples of troubleshooting and judgment.

KARBYTES, by contrast, carries a much larger philosophical, autobiographical, artistic, and archival context. Sending an employer into that entire system could make it harder—not easier—for the employer to identify the relevant evidence.

I would therefore maintain a small, conventional employment portfolio separate from the canon. That portfolio could be private, selectively shared, and adapted for different roles without changing KARBYTES every time my employment strategy changed.

### I would not want canon growth to become the reward for job-search failure

The hypothetical contains a severe emotional risk: every week without an employer response could be compensated for by publishing another set of technical exercises.

That could create an internally rewarding substitution:

> No employer recognized today’s work, but I added it to KARBYTES, so the day still produced a permanent public artifact.

There is nothing inherently false about that artifact. The problem is that it could make a nonresponsive employment process easier to tolerate indefinitely while preserving the exact conditions I needed to change.

Over time, the archive might become a monumental record of preparation for employment that never occurred. That would be meaningful in one sense, but it could also stabilize the loop of private preparation, public self-documentation, and continued external nonparticipation.

### I would want freedom to fail privately

Learning benefits from being able to produce ugly, incomplete, mistaken, or trivial work without wondering how it fits into one’s permanent public identity.

If every notebook page, code experiment, mock incident, or hardware exercise might enter KARBYTES, I could become reluctant to attempt unfamiliar material badly. I might over-polish simple work or avoid topics whose early results looked unimpressive.

Keeping most preparation private would preserve room for:

* incorrect first attempts;
* abandoned approaches;
* repetitive drills;
* crude diagrams;
* poorly written notes;
* exercises copied from instructional material;
* projects that revealed they were not worth finishing.

That private failure space would be important, especially after employment silence had already made self-evaluation difficult.

### I would not want volume to masquerade as advancement

The weekly routine could generate an enormous amount of material. Twenty months of technical drills might produce hundreds or thousands of files.

The existence of those files would demonstrate persistence, but not necessarily deepening competence. Publishing them could create a visually impressive mass that made it harder to distinguish:

* breadth from mastery;
* repetition from development;
* documentation from understanding;
* simulation from real operational experience;
* private diligence from externally tested ability.

I would rather assess progress through progressively harder problems, clean project completion, recall without notes, practical tests, and feedback from other people—not through the growing size of the canon.

### Canonization would trigger additional obligations

Adding even worthwhile technical material to canon KARBYTES would rarely be a single action. It might lead to:

* deciding the correct repository or extension pack;
* assigning filenames and dates;
* writing metadata or contextual text;
* updating directories and navigation;
* validating links;
* synchronizing copies;
* submitting pages to preservation services;
* generating new checksums;
* considering new optical-disc snapshots;
* deciding whether a new archive milestone had occurred.

The secondary archival labor could become larger than the original exercise. Keeping the preparation outside canon would let me complete a study unit, learn from it, and move on.

### I would want the evidence to be judged by someone besides me

One major problem in the hypothetical was the lack of external feedback. Putting the evidence into KARBYTES would still leave karbytes as author, curator, evaluator, and publisher.

The work would become public, but not necessarily socially tested.

What I would actually need would be review from instructors, peers, recruiters, staffing personnel, former coworkers, technical communities, or employers. Even critical feedback would provide more useful information than unilateral canonization.

Publishing privately generated evidence into my own archive could create visibility without reciprocal evaluation.

### Some academic work could eventually belong in KARBYTES

I would not impose a permanent separation between technical study and canon KARBYTES. Academic and computational material has always been compatible with the archive’s broader direction.

I would consider canonizing work when it became something more than progress evidence—for example:

* a carefully reasoned mathematical exposition;
* a genuinely useful software tool;
* an original algorithmic or philosophical analysis;
* a substantial system-design document;
* a mature technical tutorial;
* a research project with reproducible results;
* a synthesis showing how months of study changed my understanding;
* a finished work I would still consider valuable without its autobiographical context.

In that case, KARBYTES would receive the **result of the learning**, not necessarily the entire exhaust stream of preparation.

### The decision may still have been too strict

Given how central academic and technical inquiry are to KARBYTES, complete exclusion for twenty months could also have been artificial. A few finished, high-quality technical works might legitimately deserve canonization even if employment had not changed.

The better standard would not be “nothing related to preparation may enter KARBYTES.” It would be:

> Do not canonize something merely to prove that progress occurred. Canonize it when the work has independent, durable value.

So I would have kept routine study logs, synthetic exercises, application records, résumé drafts, mock interviews, and repetitive troubleshooting cases outside the canon. But I would remain open to adding a genuinely finished academic or technical contribution whose value exceeded its role as evidence that I had been keeping busy.

The underlying reason was to prevent KARBYTES from becoming a **public productivity ledger**. I would want the studying to change what I could understand and do—not merely enlarge the archive with proof that I had spent time studying.
